<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="5857f241d20749aaaf0ac28d3e31c3be"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5857f241d20749aaaf0ac28d3e31c3be">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '878d37fd-624d-4663-b34f-e5798321f54d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d45984168" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract"><p>
            <b>Peapod-like carbon-encapsulated cobalt chalcogenide nanowires</b> are designed and synthesized by a facile method. The nanowires show excellent electrochemical performance for sodium storage, suggesting that chalcogenides, especially selenides, have potential as advanced anodes for sodium-ion batteries.

            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f1ff3ac6-c122-495a-ba9e-33ae26c1fe2f/adma201600964-gra-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f1ff3ac6-c122-495a-ba9e-33ae26c1fe2f/adma201600964-gra-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/18f8669e-6ee4-48ac-a5f2-411a6d5d9849/adma201600964-gra-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f1ff3ac6-c122-495a-ba9e-33ae26c1fe2f/adma201600964-gra-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201600964-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Transition-metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reaction have attracted great attention as anodes for lithium storage because of their high theoretical capacity originating from multiple electron transfer per metal center.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0001_R_d45984160e295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma201600964-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0003_R_d45984160e295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">3</a></span> In addition, oxides and sulfides show reversible lithium capacities exceeding the theoretical bulk capacity, suggesting storage at heterophase boundaries by a “job-sharing” mechanism.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0004_R_d45984160e297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">4</a></span> These advantages triggered considerable activities in material synthesis and characterization as well as theoretical studies to address the kinetic issues of lithium storage in the conversion process,<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0005_R_d45984160e299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5</a></span> such as low reversibility, large voltage hysteresis, and poor cycle life. In the past decade, great progress has been achieved in terms of reversible capacity and cycle stability by nanostructuring and admixing conductive phases. Notwithstanding their typically lower reversible capacity for sodium storage, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are interesting not only because of low cost and abundant resource but also in view of the often surprisingly good rate performance.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0006_R_d45984160e301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a></span> Thus the exploitation of the conversion mode appears particularly intriguing.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0006_R_d45984160e303" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a></span> This is particularly worthwhile as metal oxides and sulfides display a lower redox voltage range for sodium storage than lithium storage owing to a lower thermodynamic cell voltage making these materials more suitable as anodes.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0007_R_d45984160e306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">7</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Currently, various oxides and oxide composites with carbon have been reported as useful anodes for sodium storage. However, their capacity is typically half of lithium-storage capacity and much less than the theoretical value.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0008_R_d45984160e310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a>-<a href="#adma201600964-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0011_R_d45984160e310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">11</a></span> Moreover, cycle stability<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0009_R_d45984160e312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0011_R_d45984160e312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">11</a></span> and hysteresis voltage<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0010_R_d45984160e314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0012_R_d45984160e314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Power Sources">12</a></span> are often even worse for sodium storage. From the point of view of ion size, Na-ion has a larger radius than the Li-ion, one expects conversion reaction kinetics of sodium storage to be aggravated by a more severe volume change on cycling. At a first glance one expects all kinetic factors to be worse for SIB, yet often transport within the electrodes as well as the transfer resistance from electrolyte into electrode is superior for SIB as compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0013_R_d45984160e316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0014_R_d45984160e316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Lett.">14</a></span> At any rate, a rational electrode structure design is very demanding. Furthermore, very recent studies indicate that the crystalline Na<sub>2</sub>O layer formed at the beginning of sodiation may block further transport of sodium ions, leading to a lower reversible capacity.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0015_R_d45984160e321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett. Acc. Chem. Res. Nano Energy">15</a></span> Possibly discouraged by the poor sodium-storage performance of most oxides, less attention has been paid to metal chalcogenides, especially selenides, as anodes for sodium storage. In spite of increased toxicity, the larger molar volume of latter renders them interesting in terms of sodium storage and potential to enhance our fundamental understanding of behaviors of sodium storage for the electrode materials based on conversion mechanism.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we present, for the first time, peapod-like carbon-encapsulated cobalt chalcogenide nanowires (NWs), denoted as CoS⊂carbon NWs and CoSe⊂carbon NWs, as anodes for sodium storage. This unique nanoarchitecture design offers multiple advantages for a number of critical issues associated with conversion reaction mechanism (schematic diagram in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0001">1</a>a). The 1D morphology of carbon nanowires allows for quick ion access and via 3D percolation also for quick electron access. The external nanosized carbon wall can enhance the mechanical strength of the nanowires and the internal porous carbon can accommodate the volume change of the active nanoparticles resulting in structure stability on cycling. This mechanical decoupling is also important as to avoiding Ostwald ripening. The small size of the active nanoparticles shortens the electron/Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion length in the crystal. In agreement with the expectations, the cobalt chalcogenides with peapod-like nanoarchitecture show excellent electrochemical performance. More importantly, a comparison of the sodium-storage behavior of cobalt chalcogenides and oxides demonstrates that cobalt chalcogenides exhibit a better electrochemical performance than oxides with the same nanoarchitecture. Especially CoSe⊂carbon NWs show a high reversible capacity, stable cycling, and low cycling voltage hysteresis.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201600964-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a510766-46e8-42ab-b4bf-1f6899071ed7/adma201600964-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a510766-46e8-42ab-b4bf-1f6899071ed7/adma201600964-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f9215661-76aa-4ffa-bbf8-08713436dff9/adma201600964-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a510766-46e8-42ab-b4bf-1f6899071ed7/adma201600964-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201600964-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201600964" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of peapod-like nanoarchitecture and (b) schematic illustration of the synthesis process of peapod-like CoS⊂carbon NWs and CoSe⊂carbon NWs. XRD patterns of the (c) Co⊂carbon, (d) CoS⊂carbon, and (e) CoSe⊂carbon NWs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0001">1</a>b describes the synthesis of peapod-like carbon-encapsulated cobalt chalcogenide nanowires, involving three main steps. First of all, a hydrothermal process was applied to synthesize cobalt-nitrilotriacetic acid (Co-NTC) NWs, in which Co ion is uniformly distributed providing a homogeneous metal source for cobalt chalcogenide. The Co-NTC precursor was further coated by a hydrothermal carbon shell. These core–shell-structured Co-NTC@hydrothermal carbon NWs were transformed into the desired peapod-like structured Co⊂carbon NWs after annealing by high temperature. During the annealing process, the hydrothermal-derived carbon shell was pyrolyzed into the amorphous carbon walls of the NWs, the NTC was pyrolyzed into the low density porous carbon cores and the Co-ion was reduced into metal nanoparticles embedded in the porous carbon cores. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of Co⊂carbon NWs shows a diffraction peak at 2<i>θ</i> = 44.2°, corresponding to the (111) plane of cubic Co (JCPD card No. 00-015-0806), as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0001">1</a>c. The metal Co nanoparticles have a high chemical activity and thus easily react with S and Se permeating through the carbon layers at high temperature to form the desired CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs. Their XRD analyses given in Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0001">1</a>d,e confirm that CoS and CoSe crystals correspond to hexagonal (JCPD card No. 96-900-8885) and hexagonal (JCPD card No. 96-900-8887) phases. No other diffraction peaks are detected. Obviously, this “top-down” synthesis approach represents a facile technique for constructing the complex peapod-like nanoarchitectures. The approach is different from the previously reported template-assisted method for fabricating nanopeapods, which requires an energy-consuming template removing process.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0016_R_d45984160e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16</a></span></p>
         
         <p>The morphologies and microstructures of Co⊂carbon, CoS⊂carbon, and CoSe⊂carbon NWs were investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0002">2</a> shows their representative SEM images at different magnification, revealing typical peapod-like nanoarchitectures. At low magnification, a large number of randomly oriented carbon NWs are observed with tens of micrometers in length. The higher-magnification SEM images exhibit that CoS and CoSe nanoparticles are uniformly embedded into the carbon NWs with diameters ranging from 100 to 200 nm. As to the Co⊂carbon NWs precursor, no Co nanoparticles are found within the carbon NWs because the size of nanoparticles is too small to detect them even in higher-magnification SEM images. To further elucidate its detailed nanoarchitecture, TEM is used, which indeed reveals that Co nanodots with a diameter range of several nanometers are embedded in the low-density porous carbon cores, which are themselves coated by an average wall thickness of ≈25 nm of amorphous carbon. The CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs show a similar nanoarchitecture with the diameter of CoS and CoSe nanocrystals ranging from 10 to 40 nm, greater than that of Co nanoparticles because of crystal growth in the reaction process with Se and S.High-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) images show clear lattice fringes of Co, CoS, and CoSe nanocrystals, verifying their high degree of crystallinity (Figure S1, Supporting Information). The outside carbon shell does not display clear fringes in the HR-TEM images (Figure S1d, Supporting Information), indicating amorphous carbon, which is favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion. This novel peapod-like nanoarchitecture is different from the previously reported nanopeapods fabricated by template-assisted method, in which nanocrystals are directly located on hollow carbon shells.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0016_R_d45984160e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16</a></span> In our nanoarchitecture, nanocrystals are uniformly embedded in porous carbon cores which not only accommodate their expansion on cycling but also prevent them from aggregating along the carbon shell.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201600964-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c824468c-f7ba-4a35-a0eb-8120761fb0d4/adma201600964-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c824468c-f7ba-4a35-a0eb-8120761fb0d4/adma201600964-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/288c97bc-272c-4ef8-aca9-b81f7977f830/adma201600964-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c824468c-f7ba-4a35-a0eb-8120761fb0d4/adma201600964-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201600964-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201600964" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images with different magnification of the (a,b) Co⊂carbon, (e,f) CoS⊂carbon NWs, and (i,j) CoSe⊂carbon NWs; TEM images of the(c) Co⊂carbon, (g) CoS⊂carbon, and (k) CoSe⊂carbon NWs, clearly showing the peapod-like nanoarchitecture. Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms and pore size distribution curves of the (d) Co⊂carbon, (h) CoS⊂carbon, and (l) CoSe⊂carbon NWs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Nitrogen isotherm adsorption measurements were carried out to study the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of the peapod-like nanoarchitecture (Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0002">2</a>d,h,j). The Co⊂carbon NWs show a BET surface area as high as 257 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> owing to the porous structure of carbon cores. Compared to the Co⊂carbon NWs precursor, the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs samples display a lower surface area because partial original pores are occupied by elemental S or Se. Notably, the surface areas of the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs still reach 182 and 185 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> with corresponding total pore volumes of 0.32 and 0.25 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Their pore sizes are mainly in the range of 1–20 nm based on the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda evaluation, indicating two types of pores (micropore and mesopore) in the porous carbon cores. These pores are required to accommodate the volume charge of CoS and CoSe nanocrystals on cycling, but do not excessively reduce the tap density of electrodes. On the other hand, porosity can enable Na<sup>+</sup> easily access to active mass to achieve good rate capability, which has been confirmed in previous studies.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0017_R_d45984160e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater. Nano Lett. Nano Lett. Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></span> To further investigate the interfacial area between the nanocrystals and the porous carbon, the nanocrystals were removed by an acid-etching process. Figure S2 (Supporting Information) displays the SEM images of the obtained porous carbon NWs after etching. These carbon NWs preserve the original morphology, suggesting good mechanical strength. The porous carbon NWs derived from the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs samples exhibit surface areas of 415 and 419 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, meaning that the interfacial contact areas for CoS and CoSe nanocrystals are as high as 230 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. The high interfacial contact area is able to accelerate charge transfer, which is a prerequisite for the excellent rate capability. The carbon layers surrounding the nanocrystals play an important role on electron transfer during the charge–discharge process, and are analyzed by Raman spectra. As shown in Figure S2f (Supporting Information), two typical D and G bands around 1333 and 1604 cm<sup>−1</sup> are observed for the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs samples, corresponding to sp<sup>3</sup>-type disordered carbon and sp<sup>2</sup>-type graphitized carbon, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0002_R_d45984160e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0018_R_d45984160e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></span> The ratio of D to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) for the two samples is less than 0.9, indicating larger amount of sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon than sp<sup>3</sup>-type carbon, which results in high electronic conductivity necessary for an excellent rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>In order to study the sodium-storage behavior of the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs, they were used as working electrodes in coin cells with metal sodium film as counter electrode and with NaClO<sub>4</sub>/propylene carbonate solution with 5 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate as electrolyte. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0003">3</a>a displays their initial cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The scan of the CoS⊂carbon NWs electrode shows a reduction peak at 0.65 V and an oxidation peak at 1.78 V, corresponding to the redox behavior of the conversion reaction of CoS (CoS + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>−</sup> → Co + Na<sub>2</sub>S). For the CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrode, the reduction and oxidation peaks are located at 0.77 and 1.82 V, respectively, with a narrow width. This pair of sharp redox peaks ascribes to the conversion reaction of CoSe (CoSe + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>−</sup> ↔ Co + Na<sub>2</sub>Se). The electrode shows a polarization voltage of 0.53 V, lower than that of CoS, suggesting better kinetics in the initial cycle. Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0003">3</a>b displays the charge–discharge profiles of CoS⊂carbon NWs electrode with a cutoff voltage of 0.005–3 V at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial discharge and charge capacities are 745 and 401 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, yielding a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 54%. The CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrode delivers a discharge capacity of 600 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 354 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a CE of 59%. It should be noted that the above capacity is calculated based on the mass of composite (carbon and active nanocrystals). The irreversible capacities for the initial cycle are mainly attributed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface, which could be optimized by using the presodiation route.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0019_R_d45984160e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Lett.">19</a></span> Figure S3 (Supporting Information) displays the initial discharge and charge profiles of highly purity porous carbon NWs without nanocrystal. The charge capacities of carbon NWs after the removal of CoS and CoSe nanocrystals are 131 and 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. If the capacity contribution from the carbon NWs is eliminated, CoS nanocrystals show a reversible capacity of 581 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, close to the theoretical capacity of 589 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The reversible capacity of CoSe reaches 441 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, slightly higher than the theoretical capacity of 390 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with excess capacity possibly stored in the interfacial area.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0004_R_d45984160e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">4</a></span> These results demonstrate the excellent reversibility of the cobalt chalcogenides. For comparison, the peapod-like Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs were synthesized by a similar synthesis process with a Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> loading of 70 wt%. Figure S4 (Supporting Information) displays the SEM images and XRD pattern of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs. They also show the same peapod-like nanoarchitecture, and their XRD diffraction peaks are indexed to cubic Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPD card No. 00-001-1152). Compared to the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs show a lower initial CE of 50%, delivering a reversible capacity of 377 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when used as anode in SIBs, much lower than the theoretical capacity of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. Interestingly, for lithium storage, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs deliver an initial CE of 65% and a high reversible capacity of 768 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, about twice as high as their sodium capacity. On contrast, the CoS⊂carbon electrode delivers a lithium capacity of 623 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (1.55-fold that of sodium storage) with an initial CE of 64% while the CoSe⊂carbon electrode delivers a corresponding capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (1.44-fold that of sodium storage) with an initial CE of 63%. Table S1 (Supporting Information) summarizes and compares these results, demonstrating that the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs exhibit better reversibility than the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs for sodium storage, although the latter has higher theoretical capacity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201600964-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/52a1710f-506c-4fed-a657-fb07b42dbd0b/adma201600964-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/52a1710f-506c-4fed-a657-fb07b42dbd0b/adma201600964-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff435762-58b1-4a42-bbe2-d03f687f5c69/adma201600964-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/52a1710f-506c-4fed-a657-fb07b42dbd0b/adma201600964-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201600964-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201600964" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs. Initial discharge–charge profiles of the CoS⊂carbon NWs for (b) sodium and (c) lithium storage at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Initial discharge–charge profiles of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs for (d) sodium and (e) lithium storage at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Initial discharge–charge profiles of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs for (f) sodium and (g) lithium storage at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Cycling performance of the CoS⊂carbon, CoSe⊂carbon, and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs and the CoS/super P composite at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Let us concentrate on the discharge–charge profiles of the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs for sodium/lithium storage again. Remarkably, the initial profiles of CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrode show well-defined and flat discharge plateaus around 0.92 V with corresponding charge plateaus around 1.69 V, delivering a discharge/charge polarization voltage of 0.39 V. The flat discharge plateau is attributed to the phase decomposition of CoSe nanocrystals (CoSe nanocrystals are transformed into Co metal and Na<sub>2</sub>Se), which is consistent with the CV result. For the second cycle, the charge plateaus shift to 1.26 V, corresponding to a smaller polarization voltage of 0.23 V, which is comparable to that (0.29 V) of lithium storage of (Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0003">3</a>e) and lower than those (&gt;0.5 V) of previously reported oxides.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0011_R_d45984160e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">11</a></span> Such flat initial discharge–charge plateaus are rarely observed in previous studies of oxides and chalcogenides,<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0010_R_d45984160e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0012_R_d45984160e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Power Sources">12</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0020_R_d45984160e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">20</a>-<a href="#adma201600964-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0022_R_d45984160e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nanoscale">22</a></span> demonstrating excellent kinetics of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs for sodium storage. For the CoS⊂carbon and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon electrodes, two “sloped” discharge–charge plateaus are observed for the conversion reaction of CoS nanocrystal while none of the plateaus corresponds to the conversion reaction of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocrystals in the process of sodiation (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocrystals are transformed into Co metal and Na<sub>2</sub>O). In contrast to the behavior of sodium storage, the initial discharge–charge profile of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon electrodes for lithium storage exhibits a flat discharge plateau at 1.04 V, and a “sloped” charge plateau around 2.08 V, indicating that the poor kinetics is responsible for the plateau disappearance in the process of sodium inserting/deserting. The CoSe nanocrystals obviously exhibit better kinetics for sodium storage compared to CoS and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0003">3</a>h displays the cycling performance of the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The CoS⊂carbon NWs electrodes show a stable cycle performance after two cycles with a reversible capacity of 381 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>; the capacity reaches a value as high as 294 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with CE of 99% after 100 cycles, delivering a capacity retention of 77%. Compared to the CoS⊂carbon NWs, the CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrodes exhibit better cycling stability. The reversible capacities are 340 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 299 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2 and 100 cycles, respectively, corresponding to retention of 88%. This remarkable cycling stability is better than those of previously reported oxides and chalcogenides with the same electrolyte,<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0010_R_d45984160e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0020_R_d45984160e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">20</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0023_R_d45984160e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>-<a href="#adma201600964-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0025_R_d45984160e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></span> and can be ascribed to the novel peapod-like nanoarchitecture. As a comparison, we synthesized CoS nanoparticles by a hydrothermal method. Figure S5 (Supporting Information) shows the XRD pattern and SEM image of CoS nanoparticles. All diffraction peaks are indexed to hexagonal CoS and their diameter ranges from ≈40 to ≈60 nm. The CoS/carbon black (super P) composites display a fast decay of reversible capacity, delivering a capacity of 358 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after two cycles. The capacity, however, is only 51 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, much lower than that of the CoS⊂carbon NWs (Figure S5d, Supporting Information). In comparison with the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon NWs, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs show a worse cycling stability, delivering reversible capacities of 343 and 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2 and 100 cycles, respectively, corresponding to a poor retention of 51%. To further demonstrate the remarkable cycling stability of the CoSe⊂carbon and CoS⊂carbon NW electrodes, we cycled the electrodes for more cycles at a higher current rate of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure S6 (Supporting Information), the CoSe⊂carbon NW electrodes show a stable cycle performance after two cycles with a charge capacity of 318 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the reversible capacity still reaches 266 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of 84%. For the CoS⊂carbon NWs, it delivers a reversible capacity as high as 348 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the third cycle and a capacity of 259 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles with a capacity retention of 74%.</p>
         
         <p>Cycling voltage stability is an important criterion for practical application of electrode materials. For the electrode materials based on conversion mechanism, previous studies indicate that the cycling process usually increases polarization voltages and causes the disappearance of initial charge–discharge plateaus.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0001_R_d45984160e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></span> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0004">4</a>a–c shows the discharge–charge profiles of CoS⊂carbon, CoSe⊂carbon, and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs electrodes as a function of cycling. Notably, the discharge and charge plateaus of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs are still preserved in the 100th cycle. Taking the voltage at half of the reversible capacity in the third cycle as reference, the CoS⊂carbon NWs electrode show a voltage gap (Δ<i>V</i>) of 6 mV for the 10th cycle, and the Δ<i>V</i> value increases to 44 and 121 mV for the 40th and 100th cycles. For the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs electrode, the Δ<i>V</i> values are 0.15, 0.475, and 1.493 V for the 10th, 40th, and 100th cycles, respectively, delivering a large Δ<i>V</i> change with increasing cycle number. In contrast, the CoSe⊂carbon NWs exhibit a Δ<i>V</i> of 10 mV on the 40th cycle. For the 100th cycle, the Δ<i>V</i> value has shifted to 40 mV, lower than that of the CoS⊂carbon NWs electrode and much lower than that of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs electrode, demonstrating its excellent cycling kinetics. This detailed comparison of the sodium storage behavior of cobalt chalcogenides and oxides reveals the superiority of the former.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201600964-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a19c2078-0c40-465c-9de4-5c3b35fdf2c0/adma201600964-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a19c2078-0c40-465c-9de4-5c3b35fdf2c0/adma201600964-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f9f9aa4d-43d2-4cc0-a322-0b2b8001d536/adma201600964-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a19c2078-0c40-465c-9de4-5c3b35fdf2c0/adma201600964-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201600964-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201600964" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Galvanostatic discharge–charge profiles of the (a) CoS⊂carbon, (b) CoSe⊂carbon, and (c) Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs on the various cycle(3rd, 10th, 20th, 40th, 60th, 80th, 100th) at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Rate performance and corresponding discharge–charge profiles of the (d,e) CoS⊂carbon,(f,g) CoSe⊂carbon, and (h,i) Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The peapod-like cobalt chalcogenide nanowires show an excellent rate performance as well, given in Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0004">4</a>d–g. The reversible capacities of the CoS⊂carbon NWs electrode are 379, 350, 316, 298, 285, 265, and 235 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The CoSe⊂carbon NWs electrode delivers capacities of 350, 327, and 308 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, and 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. At the high current density of 2 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacities are as high as 268 and 241 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, slightly higher than those of the CoS⊂carbon NWs. After high rate cycling, the capacity returns to 327 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and remains stable. This good rate performance is better than that obtained for sulfides and oxides anodes in previous studies.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0009_R_d45984160e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0010_R_d45984160e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a>, <a href="#adma201600964-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0020_R_d45984160e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Power Sources">20</a></span> For the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs electrode, one observes capacities of 341 and 264 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the low current rate of 0.1 and 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Yet, for high rates (2 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacities dramatically decrease (140 and 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0004">4</a>h,i.</p>
         
         <p>To explore the potential of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs for practical applications, we synthesized carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> composites,<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0026_R_d45984160e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></span> which worked as the cathode to match the CoSe⊂carbon NW anode to form a full cell. NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> material was selected as a promising cathode material owing to its fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion and flat voltage plateaus. As shown in Figure S7 (Supporting Information), XRD pattern of the prepared sample indicates that all the diffraction peaks are indexed into hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (JCPD card No. 96-222-5133). Figure S8 (Supporting Information) shows the initial discharge/charge profiles of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> composites between 2.3 and 3.9 V at a rate of 0.2 C (1C corresponds to a current density of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The composites deliver an initial discharge capacity of 97 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a CE of 99.7%. Figure S9a (Supporting Information) exhibits the charge and discharge profiles of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs//carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell between 0.5 and 3 V at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The full cells show a discharge capacity of 309 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the mass of the CoSe⊂carbon NWs with an average output voltage of about 1.7 V. It is necessary to couple with the cathode materials with higher average discharge voltage (e.g., Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>) to realize a higher output voltage (more than 2 V). After 20 cycles, the discharge capacity still remains 279 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 91% (Figure S9b, Supporting Information). In addition, the energy density of the assemble full cell is estimated to be about 115 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the entire mass of active materials of both electrodes). This value is comparable to or better than the previously reported full batteries, such as rGO-Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>//Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and ST-NTO//Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> systems.<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0027_R_d45984160e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></span></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The high reversibility, low hysteresis voltage, excellent rate capability, and stable cycling behavior of the peapod-like cobalt chalcogenide nanowires are attributed to their intrinsic properties and the unique peapod-like nanoarchitecture. Compared to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> crystal, CoS and CoSe crystals have larger interstices, which is beneficial for electron/Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. To better understand their sodium storage behavior, we performed an impedance analysis (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0005">5</a>a). All Nyquist plots display a depressed semicircle in the high-frequency region and an oblique line in the low-frequency region, which are associated with the charge-transfer process and mass transfer of sodium, respectively. Notably, the charge-transfer resistances of the CoSe⊂carbon NW and CoS⊂carbon electrodes are much lower than that of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NW electrodes. The low-frequency Warburg impedance can be used to determine the chemical diffusion coefficient <img alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201600964:adma201600964-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/50a7ba94-7d9b-4a01-a60c-e15173ece861/adma201600964-math-0001.png">) of sodium in the working electrode by the following equation<span><a href="#adma201600964-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201600964-bib-0028_R_d45984160e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">28</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="adma201600964-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/a407965c-f713-4956-9eb4-4fbd2589d3d8/adma201600964-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201600964:adma201600964-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201600964:adma201600964-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>where <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> is the molar volume, <i>S</i> is the surface area of the electrode, and <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant. δ<i>E</i>/δ<i>x</i> is the slope of the electrode potential <i>E</i> versus composition <i>x</i> (here, initial 0.1 mol Na for per mol nanocrystals) and <i>σ</i> is the Warburg factor which is obtained from the slope of <i>Z</i><sub>real</sub> versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> (Figure S10, Supporting Information). <img alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201600964:adma201600964-math-0003" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/83a8a90c-2182-481d-ad43-b46676f5debd/adma201600964-math-0003.png"> values are 1.1 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and 7.7 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for the CoSe⊂carbon and CoS⊂carbon NW electrodes, respectively, much higher than the one (5.4 × 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) of the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NW electrode.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201600964-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87db1d5c-f125-48e9-9764-c5878bc5319b/adma201600964-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/87db1d5c-f125-48e9-9764-c5878bc5319b/adma201600964-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6ee8e7cb-5549-45e0-9388-037cee62753d/adma201600964-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87db1d5c-f125-48e9-9764-c5878bc5319b/adma201600964-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201600964-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201600964" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Electrochemical impedance spectra of the CoS⊂carbon, CoSe⊂carbon, and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>⊂carbon NWs and the CoS/super P composite.b) Schematic illustration of a peapod-like nanoarchitecture for sodium storage processes. SEM images of the (c) CoS⊂carbon and (d) CoSe⊂carbon NW electrodes after 100 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The peapod-like nanoarchitectures play an important role for electron/Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport. The carbon NWs provide a highly conductive network for electron transfer, and the thin carbon shells and porous carbon cores facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion. The charge-transfer resistance of the CoS⊂carbon NWs is significantly lower than that of CoS/carbon black composites. In addition, the thin carbon shells increase the mechanical strength; the porous carbon cores prevent the nanocrystals from agglomerating and accommodate the volume change on cycling to keep the electrode integration, leading to remarkable cycle stability (Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0005">5</a>b). As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201600964-fig-0005">5</a>c,d, the NWs architectures are still preserved for the CoS⊂carbon and CoSe⊂carbon electrodes after 100 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>In conclusion, peapod-like carbon-encapsulated cobalt chalcogenide nanowires have been successfully synthesized by a facile approach without template assistance. They show excellent electrochemical performance as anodes in SIBs. This novel nanoarchitecture design offers multiple advantages with regard to the kinetic and volume problems on cycling, providing a new pathway for developing advanced anodes based conversion mechanism for sodium storage. A comprehensive comparative study of cobalt chalcogenides and oxides with peapod-like nanoarchitecture demonstrates major advantages of the cobalt chalcogenides over the oxides in terms of reversibility, hysteresis voltage, rate capability, and cycle stability. These findings not only show the huge potential of peapod-like carbon-encapsulated cobalt chalcogenides as anodes for sodium storage but also demonstrate chalcogenides, especially selenides, open new opportunities to develop advanced negative electrodes in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
